<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Metabolism</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Cell metabolism" redirects here. For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/Cell_Metabolism" title="Cell Metabolism">Cell Metabolism</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the architectural movement, see <a href="/wiki/Metabolism_(architecture)" title="Metabolism (architecture)">Metabolism (architecture)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The set of life-sustaining chemical transformations within the cells of organisms </div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ATP-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="766" data-file-width="1100" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/ATP-3D-vdW.png/220px-ATP-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/ATP-3D-vdW.png/330px-ATP-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/ATP-3D-vdW.png/440px-ATP-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ATP-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), a central intermediate in energy metabolism</div></div></div>
<p><b>Metabolism</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">μεταβολή</span> <i>metabolē</i>, "change") is the set of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>-sustaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_reactions" title="Chemical reactions">chemical reactions</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisms" title="Organisms">organisms</a>. The three main purposes of metabolism are the conversion of food/fuel to energy to run cellular processes, the conversion of food/fuel to building blocks for <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, and the elimination of <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_waste#Nitrogen_wastes" title="Metabolic waste">nitrogenous wastes</a>. These <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>-catalyzed reactions allow organisms to grow and reproduce, maintain their structures, and respond to their environments. The word metabolism can also refer to the sum of all chemical reactions that occur in living organisms, including <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a> and the transport of substances into and between different cells, in which case the set of reactions within the cells is called <b>intermediary metabolism</b> or <b>intermediate metabolism</b>.
</p><p>Metabolism is usually divided into two categories: <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a>, the <i>breaking down</i> of organic matter for example, the breaking down of glucose to pyruvate, by <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>, the <i>building up</i> of components of cells such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>. Usually, breaking down releases <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> and building up consumes energy.
</p><p>The chemical reactions of metabolism are organized into <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a>, in which one chemical is transformed through a series of steps into another chemical, by a sequence of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. Enzymes are crucial to metabolism because they allow organisms to drive desirable reactions that require <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> that will not occur by themselves, by <a href="/wiki/Coupling_(physics)" title="Coupling (physics)">coupling</a> them to <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_process" title="Spontaneous process">spontaneous reactions</a> that release energy. Enzymes act as <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> that allow the reactions to proceed more rapidly. Enzymes also allow the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway#Regulation" title="Metabolic pathway">regulation</a> of metabolic pathways in response to changes in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cell's</a> environment or to <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">signals</a> from other cells.
</p><p>The metabolic system of a particular organism determines which substances it will find <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutritious</a> and which <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisonous</a>. For example, some <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> use <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> as a nutrient, yet this gas is poisonous to animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physiology1_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physiology1-1">[1]</a></sup> The speed of metabolism, the <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">metabolic rate</a>, influences how much food an organism will require, and also affects how it is able to obtain that food.
</p><p>A striking feature of metabolism is the similarity of the basic metabolic pathways and components between even vastly different species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> For example, the set of <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acids</a> that are best known as the intermediates in the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a> are present in all known organisms, being found in species as diverse as the <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular</a> bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i> and huge <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> like <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithE_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-SmithE-3">[3]</a></sup> These striking similarities in metabolic pathways are likely due to their early appearance in <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">evolutionary history</a>, and their retention because of their <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">efficacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebenhoh_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebenhoh-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cascante_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cascante-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Key_biochemicals">Key biochemicals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Key biochemicals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">Biomolecule</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">Cell (biology)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">Biochemistry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="893" data-file-width="1100" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png/250px-Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png/375px-Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png/500px-Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trimyristin-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triacylglycerol" title="Triacylglycerol">triacylglycerol</a> lipid</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1792" data-file-width="2054" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg/220px-Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg/330px-Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg/440px-Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_Metabolism_-_Pathways.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This is a diagram depicting a large set of human metabolic pathways.</div></div></div>
<p>Most of the structures that make up animals, plants and microbes are made from three basic classes of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a>: <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> (often called <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>). As these molecules are vital for life, metabolic reactions either focus on making these molecules during the construction of cells and tissues, or by breaking them down and using them as a source of energy, by their digestion. These biochemicals can be joined together to make <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, essential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macromolecules" title="Macromolecules">macromolecules</a> of life.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amino_acids_and_proteins">Amino acids and proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Amino acids and proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">Proteins</a> are made of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> arranged in a linear chain joined together by <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bonds</a>. Many proteins are <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> that <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyze</a> the chemical reactions in metabolism. Other proteins have structural or mechanical functions, such as those that form the <a href="/wiki/Cytoskeleton" title="Cytoskeleton">cytoskeleton</a>, a system of <a href="/wiki/Scaffolding" title="Scaffolding">scaffolding</a> that maintains the cell shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Proteins are also important in <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">cell signaling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">immune responses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_adhesion" title="Cell adhesion">cell adhesion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a> across membranes, and the <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-7">[7]</a></sup> Amino acids also contribute to cellular energy metabolism by providing a carbon source for entry into the citric acid cycle (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tricarboxylic_acid_cycle" title="Tricarboxylic acid cycle">tricarboxylic acid cycle</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> especially when a primary source of energy, such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, is scarce, or when cells undergo metabolic stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lipids">Lipids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lipids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">Lipids</a> are the most diverse group of biochemicals. Their main structural uses are as part of <a href="/wiki/Biological_membrane" title="Biological membrane">biological membranes</a> both internal and external, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a>, or as a source of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-7">[7]</a></sup> Lipids are usually defined as <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphiphiles" title="Amphiphiles">amphipathic</a> biological molecules but will dissolve in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_solvent" title="Organic solvent">organic solvents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a> are a large group of compounds that contain <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>; a glycerol molecule attached to three fatty acid <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> is called a <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triacylglyceride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Several variations on this basic structure exist, including alternate backbones such as <a href="/wiki/Sphingosine" title="Sphingosine">sphingosine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sphingolipid" title="Sphingolipid">sphingolipids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrophile" title="Hydrophile">hydrophilic</a> groups such as <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> as in <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a>. <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">Steroids</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> are another major class of lipids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates">Carbohydrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:233px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif"><img alt="The straight chain form consists of four C H O H groups linked in a row, capped at the ends by an aldehyde group C O H and a methanol group C H 2 O H. To form the ring, the aldehyde group combines with the O H group of the next-to-last carbon at the other end, just before the methanol group." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="181" data-file-width="231" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" width="231"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glucose_Fisher_to_Haworth.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a> can exist in both a straight-chain and ring form.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">Carbohydrates</a> are <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a>, with many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> groups attached, that can exist as straight chains or rings. Carbohydrates are the most abundant biological molecules, and fill numerous roles, such as the storage and transport of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>) and structural components (<a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in plants, <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> in animals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-7">[7]</a></sup> The basic carbohydrate units are called <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> and include <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>, and most importantly <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>. Monosaccharides can be linked together to form <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> in almost limitless ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleotides">Nucleotides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nucleotides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The two nucleic acids, DNA and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, are polymers of <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>. Each nucleotide is composed of a phosphate attached to a <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a> or <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> sugar group which is attached to a <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenous_base" title="Nitrogenous base">nitrogenous base</a>. Nucleic acids are critical for the storage and use of genetic information, and its interpretation through the processes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">protein biosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-7">[7]</a></sup> This information is protected by <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> mechanisms and propagated through <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> have an <a href="/wiki/RNA_virus" title="RNA virus">RNA genome</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>, which uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverse_transcription" title="Reverse transcription">reverse transcription</a> to create a DNA template from its viral RNA genome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> RNA in <a href="/wiki/Ribozyme" title="Ribozyme">ribozymes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Spliceosome" title="Spliceosome">spliceosomes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> is similar to enzymes as it can catalyze chemical reactions. Individual <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleosides</a> are made by attaching a <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobase</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a> sugar. These bases are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterocyclic" title="Heterocyclic">heterocyclic</a> rings containing nitrogen, classified as <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purines</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidines</a>. Nucleotides also act as coenzymes in metabolic-group-transfer reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wimmer_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wimmer-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coenzymes">Coenzymes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Coenzymes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="187" data-file-width="512" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg/300px-Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg/450px-Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg/600px-Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acetyl-CoA-2D.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzyme</a> <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a>.The transferable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetyl" title="Acetyl">acetyl group</a> is bonded to the sulfur atom at the extreme left.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">Coenzyme</a></div>
<p>Metabolism involves a vast array of chemical reactions, but most fall under a few basic types of reactions that involve the transfer of <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional groups</a> of atoms and their bonds within molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This common chemistry allows cells to use a small set of metabolic intermediates to carry chemical groups between different reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wimmer_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wimmer-15">[15]</a></sup> These group-transfer intermediates are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzymes</a>. Each class of group-transfer reactions is carried out by a particular coenzyme, which is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_(biochemistry)" title="Substrate (biochemistry)">substrate</a> for a set of enzymes that produce it, and a set of enzymes that consume it. These coenzymes are therefore continuously made, consumed and then recycled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>One central coenzyme is <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the universal energy currency of cells. This nucleotide is used to transfer chemical energy between different chemical reactions. There is only a small amount of ATP in cells, but as it is continuously regenerated, the human body can use about its own weight in ATP per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-17">[17]</a></sup> ATP acts as a bridge between <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>. Catabolism breaks down molecules, and anabolism puts them together. Catabolic reactions generate ATP, and anabolic reactions consume it. It also serves as a carrier of phosphate groups in <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylation</a> reactions.
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamin</a> is an organic compound needed in small quantities that cannot be made in cells. In human <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>, most vitamins function as coenzymes after modification; for example, all water-soluble vitamins are phosphorylated or are coupled to nucleotides when they are used in cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide">Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide</a> (NAD<sup>+</sup>), a derivative of vitamin B<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">niacin</a>), is an important coenzyme that acts as a hydrogen acceptor. Hundreds of separate types of <a href="/wiki/Dehydrogenase" title="Dehydrogenase">dehydrogenases</a> remove electrons from their substrates and <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduce</a> NAD<sup>+</sup> into NADH. This reduced form of the coenzyme is then a substrate for any of the <a href="/wiki/Reductase" title="Reductase">reductases</a> in the cell that need to reduce their substrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide exists in two related forms in the cell, NADH and NADPH. The NAD<sup>+</sup>/NADH form is more important in catabolic reactions, while NADP<sup>+</sup>/NADPH is used in anabolic reactions.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/300px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/450px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/1GZX_Haemoglobin.png/600px-1GZX_Haemoglobin.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1GZX_Haemoglobin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>. The protein subunits are in red and blue, and the iron-containing <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> groups in green. From <span class="plainlinks"><a href="/wiki/Protein_Data_Bank" title="Protein Data Bank">PDB</a>: <a class="external text" href="https://www.rcsb.org/structure/1GZX" rel="nofollow">1GZX</a></span>​.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minerals_and_cofactors">Minerals and cofactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Minerals and cofactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_metabolism" title="Metal metabolism">Metal metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bioinorganic_chemistry" title="Bioinorganic chemistry">Bioinorganic chemistry</a></div>
<p>Inorganic elements play critical roles in metabolism; some are abundant (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>) while others function at minute concentrations. About 99% of a mammal's mass is made up of the elements <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heymsfield_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heymsfield-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">Organic compounds</a> (proteins, lipids and carbohydrates) contain the majority of the carbon and nitrogen; most of the oxygen and hydrogen is present as water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heymsfield_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Heymsfield-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The abundant inorganic elements act as <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionic</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>. The most important ions are <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> and the organic ion <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>. The maintenance of precise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_gradient" title="Ion gradient">ion gradients</a> across <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a> maintains <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_pressure" title="Osmotic pressure">osmotic pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Ions are also critical for <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> function, as <a href="/wiki/Action_potential" title="Action potential">action potentials</a> in these tissues are produced by the exchange of electrolytes between the <a href="/wiki/Extracellular_fluid" title="Extracellular fluid">extracellular fluid</a> and the cell's fluid, the <a href="/wiki/Cytosol" title="Cytosol">cytosol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Electrolytes enter and leave cells through proteins in the cell membrane called <a href="/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channels</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_contraction" title="Muscle contraction">muscle contraction</a> depends upon the movement of calcium, sodium and potassium through ion channels in the cell membrane and <a href="/wiki/T-tubule" title="T-tubule">T-tubules</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">Transition metals</a> are usually present as <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a> in organisms, with <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> being most abundant of those.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Husted_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Husted-25">[25]</a></sup> These metals are used in some proteins as <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactors</a> and are essential for the activity of enzymes such as <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalase</a> and oxygen-carrier proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Metal cofactors are bound tightly to specific sites in proteins; although enzyme cofactors can be modified during catalysis, they always return to their original state by the end of the reaction catalyzed. Metal micronutrients are taken up into organisms by specific transporters and bind to storage proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Ferritin" title="Ferritin">ferritin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Metallothionein" title="Metallothionein">metallothionein</a> when not in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Catabolism">Catabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Catabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">Catabolism</a> is the set of metabolic processes that break down large molecules. These include breaking down and oxidizing food molecules. The purpose of the catabolic reactions is to provide the energy and components needed by anabolic reactions which build molecules. The exact nature of these catabolic reactions differ from organism to organism, and organisms can be classified based on their sources of energy and carbon (their <a href="/wiki/Primary_nutritional_groups" title="Primary nutritional groups">primary nutritional groups</a>), as shown in the table below. Organic molecules are used as a source of energy by <a href="/wiki/Organotroph" title="Organotroph">organotrophs</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Lithotroph" title="Lithotroph">lithotrophs</a> use inorganic substrates, and <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">phototrophs</a> capture sunlight as <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy#Chemical_potential_energy" title="Potential energy">chemical energy</a>. However, all these different forms of metabolism depend on <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reactions that involve the transfer of electrons from reduced donor molecules such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a>, water, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ferrous" title="Ferrous">ferrous ions</a> to acceptor molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In animals, these reactions involve complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a> that are broken down to simpler molecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water. In <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> organisms, such as plants and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, these electron-transfer reactions do not release energy but are used as a way of storing energy absorbed from sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson2004_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson2004-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
