<p>The most common set of catabolic reactions in animals can be separated into three main stages. In the first stage, large organic molecules, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, are digested into their smaller components outside cells. Next, these smaller molecules are taken up by cells and converted to smaller molecules, usually <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl coenzyme A</a> (acetyl-CoA), which releases some energy. Finally, the acetyl group on the CoA is oxidised to water and carbon dioxide in the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a>, releasing the energy that is stored by reducing the coenzyme <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide">nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide</a> (NAD<sup>+</sup>) into NADH.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digestion">Digestion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Digestion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">Digestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">Gastrointestinal tract</a></div>
<p>Macromolecules such as starch, cellulose or proteins cannot be rapidly taken up by cells and must be broken into their smaller units before they can be used in cell metabolism. Several common classes of enzymes digest these polymers. These <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">digestive enzymes</a> include <a href="/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">proteases</a> that digest proteins into amino acids, as well as <a href="/wiki/Glycoside_hydrolase" title="Glycoside hydrolase">glycoside hydrolases</a> that digest polysaccharides into simple sugars known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monosaccharides" title="Monosaccharides">monosaccharides</a>.
</p><p>Microbes simply secrete digestive enzymes into their surroundings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> while animals only secrete these enzymes from specialized cells in their <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract" title="Gastrointestinal tract">guts</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The amino acids or sugars released by these extracellular enzymes are then pumped into cells by <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a> proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catabolism_schematic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="425" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/300px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/450px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/600px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catabolism_schematic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified outline of the catabolism of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_from_organic_compounds">Energy from organic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Energy from organic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">Cellular respiration</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">Fermentation (biochemistry)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate_catabolism" title="Carbohydrate catabolism">Carbohydrate catabolism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat_catabolism" title="Fat catabolism">Fat catabolism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protein_catabolism" title="Protein catabolism">Protein catabolism</a></div>
<p>Carbohydrate catabolism is the breakdown of carbohydrates into smaller units. Carbohydrates are usually taken into cells once they have been digested into <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Once inside, the major route of breakdown is <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a>, where sugars such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> are converted into <a href="/wiki/Pyruvic_acid" title="Pyruvic acid">pyruvate</a> and some ATP is generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouche_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bouche-37">[37]</a></sup> Pyruvate is an intermediate in several metabolic pathways, but the majority is converted to <a href="/wiki/Acetyl-CoA" title="Acetyl-CoA">acetyl-CoA</a> through aerobic (with oxygen) glycolysis and fed into the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. Although some more ATP is generated in the citric acid cycle, the most important product is NADH, which is made from NAD<sup>+</sup> as the acetyl-CoA is oxidized. This oxidation releases <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> as a waste product. In anaerobic conditions, glycolysis produces <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>, through the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Lactate_dehydrogenase" title="Lactate dehydrogenase">lactate dehydrogenase</a> re-oxidizing NADH to NAD+ for re-use in glycolysis. An alternative route for glucose breakdown is the <a href="/wiki/Pentose_phosphate_pathway" title="Pentose phosphate pathway">pentose phosphate pathway</a>, which reduces the coenzyme <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADPH" title="NADPH">NADPH</a> and produces <a href="/wiki/Pentose" title="Pentose">pentose</a> sugars such as <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a>, the sugar component of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.
</p><p>Fats are catabolised by <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> to free fatty acids and glycerol. The glycerol enters glycolysis and the fatty acids are broken down by <a href="/wiki/Beta_oxidation" title="Beta oxidation">beta oxidation</a> to release acetyl-CoA, which then is fed into the citric acid cycle. Fatty acids release more energy upon oxidation than carbohydrates because carbohydrates contain more oxygen in their structures. Steroids are also broken down by some bacteria in a process similar to beta oxidation, and this breakdown process involves the release of significant amounts of acetyl-CoA, propionyl-CoA, and pyruvate, which can all be used by the cell for energy. <i>M. tuberculosis</i> can also grow on the lipid <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> as a sole source of carbon, and genes involved in the cholesterol use pathway(s) have been validated as important during various stages of the infection lifecycle of <i>M. tuberculosis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">Amino acids</a> are either used to synthesize proteins and other biomolecules, or oxidized to <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> and carbon dioxide as a source of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The oxidation pathway starts with the removal of the amino group by a <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminase</a>. The amino group is fed into the <a href="/wiki/Urea_cycle" title="Urea cycle">urea cycle</a>, leaving a deaminated carbon skeleton in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Keto_acid" title="Keto acid">keto acid</a>. Several of these keto acids are intermediates in the citric acid cycle, for example the deamination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> forms α-<a href="/wiki/Alpha-Ketoglutaric_acid" title="Alpha-Ketoglutaric acid">ketoglutarate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Glucogenic_amino_acid" title="Glucogenic amino acid">glucogenic amino acids</a> can also be converted into glucose, through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a> (discussed below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_transformations">Energy transformations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Energy transformations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidative_phosphorylation">Oxidative phosphorylation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Oxidative phosphorylation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">Oxidative phosphorylation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemiosmosis" title="Chemiosmosis">Chemiosmosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">Mitochondrion</a></div>
<p>In oxidative phosphorylation, the electrons removed from organic molecules in areas such as the protagon acid cycle are transferred to oxygen and the energy released is used to make ATP. This is done in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> by a series of proteins in the membranes of mitochondria called the <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a>, these proteins are found in the cell's <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_cell_structure" title="Bacterial cell structure">inner membrane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> These proteins use the energy released from passing electrons from <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reduced</a> molecules like NADH onto <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to pump <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> across a membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ATPsyn.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="300" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/ATPsyn.gif/220px-ATPsyn.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/ATPsyn.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ATPsyn.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mechanism of <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a>. ATP is shown in red, ADP and phosphate in pink and the rotating stalk subunit in black.</div></div></div>
<p>Pumping protons out of the mitochondria creates a proton <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">concentration difference</a> across the membrane and generates an <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">electrochemical gradient</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This force drives protons back into the mitochondrion through the base of an enzyme called <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a>. The flow of protons makes the stalk subunit rotate, causing the <a href="/wiki/Active_site" title="Active site">active site</a> of the synthase domain to change shape and phosphorylate <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_diphosphate" title="Adenosine diphosphate">adenosine diphosphate</a> – turning it into ATP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_from_inorganic_compounds">Energy from inorganic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Energy from inorganic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Microbial_metabolism" title="Microbial metabolism">Microbial metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">Nitrogen cycle</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemolithotroph" title="Chemolithotroph">Chemolithotrophy</a> is a type of metabolism found in <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> where energy is obtained from the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compounds</a>. These organisms can use <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> reduced <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compounds (such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiosulfate" title="Thiosulfate">thiosulfate</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physiology1_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Physiology1-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_oxide" title="Iron(II) oxide">ferrous iron (FeII)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> as sources of reducing power and they gain energy from the oxidation of these compounds with electron acceptors such as <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">nitrite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> These microbial processes are important in global <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycles</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Acetogenesis" title="Acetogenesis">acetogenesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrification" title="Nitrification">nitrification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denitrification" title="Denitrification">denitrification</a> and are critical for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_(soil)" title="Fertility (soil)">soil fertility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_from_light">Energy from light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Energy from light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Phototroph" title="Phototroph">Phototroph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photophosphorylation" title="Photophosphorylation">Photophosphorylation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">Chloroplast</a></div>
<p>The energy in sunlight is captured by <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purple_bacteria" title="Purple bacteria">purple bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_sulfur_bacteria" title="Green sulfur bacteria">green sulfur bacteria</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>. This process is often coupled to the conversion of carbon dioxide into organic compounds, as part of photosynthesis, which is discussed below. The energy capture and carbon fixation systems can however operate separately in prokaryotes, as purple bacteria and green sulfur bacteria can use sunlight as a source of energy, while switching between carbon fixation and the fermentation of organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many organisms the capture of solar energy is similar in principle to oxidative phosphorylation, as it involves the storage of energy as a proton concentration gradient. This proton motive force then drives ATP synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dimroth_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dimroth-17">[17]</a></sup> The electrons needed to drive this electron transport chain come from light-gathering proteins called <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_reaction_centre" title="Photosynthetic reaction centre">photosynthetic reaction centres</a> or <a href="/wiki/Rhodopsin" title="Rhodopsin">rhodopsins</a>. Reaction centers are classed into two types depending on the type of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_pigment" title="Photosynthetic pigment">photosynthetic pigment</a> present, with most photosynthetic bacteria only having one type, while plants and cyanobacteria have two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In plants, algae, and cyanobacteria, <a href="/wiki/Photosystem" title="Photosystem">photosystem II</a> uses light energy to remove electrons from water, releasing oxygen as a waste product. The electrons then flow to the <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b6f_complex" title="Cytochrome b6f complex">cytochrome b6f complex</a>, which uses their energy to pump protons across the <a href="/wiki/Thylakoid" title="Thylakoid">thylakoid</a> membrane in the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson2004_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson2004-30">[30]</a></sup> These protons move back through the membrane as they drive the ATP synthase, as before. The electrons then flow through <a href="/wiki/Photosystem" title="Photosystem">photosystem I</a> and can then either be used to reduce the coenzyme NADP<sup>+</sup>, for use in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a>, which is discussed below, or recycled for further ATP generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anabolism">Anabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Anabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">Anabolism</a></div>
<p><b>Anabolism</b> is the set of constructive metabolic processes where the energy released by catabolism is used to synthesize complex molecules. In general, the complex molecules that make up cellular structures are constructed step-by-step from small and simple precursors. Anabolism involves three basic stages. First, the production of precursors such as <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">isoprenoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>, secondly, their activation into reactive forms using energy from ATP, and thirdly, the assembly of these precursors into complex molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.
</p><p>Organisms differ according to the number of constructed molecules in their cells. <a href="/wiki/Autotroph" title="Autotroph">Autotrophs</a> such as plants can construct the complex organic molecules in cells such as polysaccharides and proteins from simple molecules like <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water. <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">Heterotrophs</a>, on the other hand, require a source of more complex substances, such as monosaccharides and amino acids, to produce these complex molecules. Organisms can be further classified by ultimate source of their energy: photoautotrophs and photoheterotrophs obtain energy from light, whereas chemoautotrophs and chemoheterotrophs obtain energy from inorganic oxidation reactions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_fixation">Carbon fixation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carbon fixation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">Carbon fixation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">Chemosynthesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg/220px-Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg/330px-Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg/440px-Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plagiomnium_affine_laminazellen.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plant cells (bounded by purple walls) filled with chloroplasts (green), which are the site of photosynthesis</div></div></div>
<p>Photosynthesis is the synthesis of carbohydrates from sunlight and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>). In plants, cyanobacteria and algae, oxygenic photosynthesis splits water, with oxygen produced as a waste product. This process uses the ATP and NADPH produced by the <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_reaction_centre" title="Photosynthetic reaction centre">photosynthetic reaction centres</a>, as described above, to convert CO<sub>2</sub> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerate_3-phosphate" title="Glycerate 3-phosphate">glycerate 3-phosphate</a>, which can then be converted into glucose. This carbon-fixation reaction is carried out by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">RuBisCO</a> as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin – Benson cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Three types of photosynthesis occur in plants, <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C3 carbon fixation</a>, <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 carbon fixation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crassulacean_acid_metabolism" title="Crassulacean acid metabolism">CAM photosynthesis</a>. These differ by the route that carbon dioxide takes to the Calvin cycle, with C3 plants fixing CO<sub>2</sub> directly, while C4 and CAM photosynthesis incorporate the CO<sub>2</sub> into other compounds first, as adaptations to deal with intense sunlight and dry conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In photosynthetic <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotes</a> the mechanisms of carbon fixation are more diverse. Here, carbon dioxide can be fixed by the Calvin – Benson cycle, a <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Krebs_cycle" title="Reverse Krebs cycle">reversed citric acid</a> cycle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> or the carboxylation of acetyl-CoA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Prokaryotic <a href="/wiki/Chemotroph" title="Chemotroph">chemoautotrophs</a> also fix CO<sub>2</sub> through the Calvin – Benson cycle, but use energy from inorganic compounds to drive the reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbohydrates_and_glycans">Carbohydrates and glycans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Carbohydrates and glycans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">Gluconeogenesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glyoxylate_cycle" title="Glyoxylate cycle">Glyoxylate cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycogenesis" title="Glycogenesis">Glycogenesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glycosylation" title="Glycosylation">Glycosylation</a></div>
<p>In carbohydrate anabolism, simple organic acids can be converted into <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and then used to assemble <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>. The generation of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> from compounds like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerate_3-phosphate" title="Glycerate 3-phosphate">glycerate 3-phosphate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>. Gluconeogenesis converts pyruvate to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glucose-6-phosphate" title="Glucose-6-phosphate">glucose-6-phosphate</a> through a series of intermediates, many of which are shared with <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bouche_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bouche-37">[37]</a></sup> However, this pathway is not simply <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> run in reverse, as several steps are catalyzed by non-glycolytic enzymes. This is important as it allows the formation and breakdown of glucose to be regulated separately, and prevents both pathways from running simultaneously in a <a href="/wiki/Futile_cycle" title="Futile cycle">futile cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although fat is a common way of storing energy, in <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> such as humans the <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a> in these stores cannot be converted to glucose through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a> as these organisms cannot convert acetyl-CoA into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyruvate" title="Pyruvate">pyruvate</a>; plants do, but animals do not, have the necessary enzymatic machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensign_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensign-63">[63]</a></sup> As a result, after long-term starvation, vertebrates need to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ketone_body" title="Ketone body">ketone bodies</a> from fatty acids to replace glucose in tissues such as the brain that cannot metabolize fatty acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In other organisms such as plants and bacteria, this metabolic problem is solved using the <a href="/wiki/Glyoxylate_cycle" title="Glyoxylate cycle">glyoxylate cycle</a>, which bypasses the <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a> step in the citric acid cycle and allows the transformation of acetyl-CoA to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxaloacetate" title="Oxaloacetate">oxaloacetate</a>, where it can be used for the production of glucose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensign_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ensign-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornberg_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kornberg-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polysaccharides and <a href="/wiki/Glycan" title="Glycan">glycans</a> are made by the sequential addition of monosaccharides by <a href="/wiki/Glycosyltransferase" title="Glycosyltransferase">glycosyltransferase</a> from a reactive sugar-phosphate donor such as <a href="/wiki/Uridine_diphosphate_glucose" title="Uridine diphosphate glucose">uridine diphosphate glucose</a> (UDP-glucose) to an acceptor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group on the growing polysaccharide. As any of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> groups on the ring of the substrate can be acceptors, the polysaccharides produced can have straight or branched structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The polysaccharides produced can have structural or metabolic functions themselves, or be transferred to lipids and proteins by enzymes called <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharyltransferase" title="Oligosaccharyltransferase">oligosaccharyltransferases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Fatty_acids.2C_isoprenoids_and_steroids"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fatty_acids,_isoprenoids_and_steroids">Fatty acids, isoprenoids and steroids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fatty acids, isoprenoids and steroids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_synthesis" title="Fatty acid synthesis">Fatty acid synthesis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_metabolism" title="Steroid metabolism">Steroid metabolism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sterol_synthesis.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="571" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sterol_synthesis.svg/350px-Sterol_synthesis.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sterol_synthesis.svg/525px-Sterol_synthesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sterol_synthesis.svg/700px-Sterol_synthesis.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sterol_synthesis.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simplified version of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_synthesis" title="Steroid synthesis">steroid synthesis</a> pathway with the intermediates <a href="/wiki/Isopentenyl_pyrophosphate" title="Isopentenyl pyrophosphate">isopentenyl pyrophosphate</a> (IPP), <a href="/wiki/Dimethylallyl_pyrophosphate" title="Dimethylallyl pyrophosphate">dimethylallyl pyrophosphate</a> (DMAPP), <a href="/wiki/Geranyl_pyrophosphate" title="Geranyl pyrophosphate">geranyl pyrophosphate</a> (GPP) and <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">squalene</a> shown. Some intermediates are omitted for clarity.</div></div></div>
<p>Fatty acids are made by <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_synthase" title="Fatty acid synthase">fatty acid synthases</a> that polymerize and then reduce acetyl-CoA units. The acyl chains in the fatty acids are extended by a cycle of reactions that add the acyl group, reduce it to an alcohol, <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydrate</a> it to an <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkene</a> group and then reduce it again to an <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkane</a> group. The enzymes of fatty acid biosynthesis are divided into two groups: in animals and fungi, all these fatty acid synthase reactions are carried out by a single multifunctional type I protein,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> while in plant <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a> and bacteria separate type II enzymes perform each step in the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Terpene" title="Terpene">Terpenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terpenoid" title="Terpenoid">isoprenoids</a> are a large class of lipids that include the <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> and form the largest class of plant <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> These compounds are made by the assembly and modification of <a href="/wiki/Isoprene" title="Isoprene">isoprene</a> units donated from the reactive precursors <a href="/wiki/Isopentenyl_pyrophosphate" title="Isopentenyl pyrophosphate">isopentenyl pyrophosphate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dimethylallyl_pyrophosphate" title="Dimethylallyl pyrophosphate">dimethylallyl pyrophosphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuzuyama_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuzuyama-73">[73]</a></sup> These precursors can be made in different ways. In animals and archaea, the <a href="/wiki/Mevalonate_pathway" title="Mevalonate pathway">mevalonate pathway</a> produces these compounds from acetyl-CoA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> while in plants and bacteria the <a href="/wiki/Non-mevalonate_pathway" title="Non-mevalonate pathway">non-mevalonate pathway</a> uses pyruvate and <a href="/wiki/Glyceraldehyde_3-phosphate" title="Glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate">glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate</a> as substrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuzuyama_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuzuyama-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> One important reaction that uses these activated isoprene donors is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steroid_biosynthesis" title="Steroid biosynthesis">steroid biosynthesis</a>. Here, the isoprene units are joined together to make <a href="/wiki/Squalene" title="Squalene">squalene</a> and then folded up and formed into a set of rings to make <a href="/wiki/Lanosterol" title="Lanosterol">lanosterol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroepfer_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schroepfer-76">[76]</a></sup> Lanosterol can then be converted into other steroids such as <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ergosterol" title="Ergosterol">ergosterol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroepfer_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schroepfer-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteins">Proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">Protein biosynthesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_synthesis" title="Amino acid synthesis">Amino acid synthesis</a></div>
<p>Organisms vary in their ability to synthesize the 20 common amino acids. Most bacteria and plants can synthesize all twenty, but mammals can only synthesize eleven nonessential amino acids, so nine <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a> must be obtained from food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-7">[7]</a></sup> Some simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, such as the bacteria <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma_pneumoniae" title="Mycoplasma pneumoniae">Mycoplasma pneumoniae</a></i>, lack all amino acid synthesis and take their amino acids directly from their hosts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> All amino acids are synthesized from intermediates in glycolysis, the citric acid cycle, or the pentose phosphate pathway. Nitrogen is provided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glutamate" title="Glutamate">glutamate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a>. Amino acid synthesis depends on the formation of the appropriate alpha-keto acid, which is then <a href="/wiki/Transaminase" title="Transaminase">transaminated</a> to form an amino acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Amino acids are made into proteins by being joined together in a chain of <a href="/wiki/Peptide_bond" title="Peptide bond">peptide bonds</a>. Each different protein has a unique sequence of amino acid residues: this is its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_structure" title="Primary structure">primary structure</a>. Just as the letters of the alphabet can be combined to form an almost endless variety of words, amino acids can be linked in varying sequences to form a huge variety of proteins. Proteins are made from amino acids that have been activated by attachment to a <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> molecule through an <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">ester</a> bond. This aminoacyl-tRNA precursor is produced in an <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>-dependent reaction carried out by an <a href="/wiki/Aminoacyl_tRNA_synthetase" title="Aminoacyl tRNA synthetase">aminoacyl tRNA synthetase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> This aminoacyl-tRNA is then a substrate for the <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a>, which joins the amino acid onto the elongating protein chain, using the sequence information in a <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleotide_synthesis_and_salvage">Nucleotide synthesis and salvage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nucleotide synthesis and salvage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide_salvage" title="Nucleotide salvage">Nucleotide salvage</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrimidine_biosynthesis" title="Pyrimidine biosynthesis">Pyrimidine biosynthesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Purine#Metabolism" title="Purine">Purine § Metabolism</a></div>
<p>Nucleotides are made from amino acids, carbon dioxide and <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a> in pathways that require large amounts of metabolic energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph-82">[82]</a></sup> Consequently, most organisms have efficient systems to salvage preformed nucleotides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">Purines</a> are synthesized as <a href="/wiki/Nucleoside" title="Nucleoside">nucleosides</a> (bases attached to <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid_22531138_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid_22531138-84">[84]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> are made from the precursor nucleoside <a href="/wiki/Inosine" title="Inosine">inosine</a> monophosphate, which is synthesized using atoms from the amino acids <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glutamine" title="Glutamine">glutamine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aspartic_acid" title="Aspartic acid">aspartic acid</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Formate" title="Formate">formate</a> transferred from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coenzyme" title="Coenzyme">coenzyme</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">tetrahydrofolate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">Pyrimidines</a>, on the other hand, are synthesized from the base <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrimidinecarboxylic_acid" title="Pyrimidinecarboxylic acid">orotate</a>, which is formed from glutamine and aspartate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Xenobiotics_and_redox_metabolism">Xenobiotics and redox metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Xenobiotics and redox metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenobiotic_metabolism" title="Xenobiotic metabolism">Xenobiotic metabolism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drug_metabolism" title="Drug metabolism">Drug metabolism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_metabolism" title="Alcohol metabolism">Alcohol metabolism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidant</a></div>
<p>All organisms are constantly exposed to compounds that they cannot use as foods and would be harmful if they accumulated in cells, as they have no metabolic function. These potentially damaging compounds are called <a href="/wiki/Xenobiotic" title="Xenobiotic">xenobiotics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Xenobiotics such as <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">synthetic drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">natural poisons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> are detoxified by a set of xenobiotic-metabolizing enzymes. In humans, these include <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_P450" title="Cytochrome P450">cytochrome P450 oxidases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glucuronosyltransferase" title="Glucuronosyltransferase">UDP-glucuronosyltransferases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Glutathione_S-transferase" title="Glutathione S-transferase">glutathione <i>S</i>-transferases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> This system of enzymes acts in three stages to firstly oxidize the xenobiotic (phase I) and then conjugate water-soluble groups onto the molecule (phase II). The modified water-soluble xenobiotic can then be pumped out of cells and in multicellular organisms may be further metabolized before being excreted (phase III). In <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, these reactions are particularly important in microbial <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a> of pollutants and the <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a> of contaminated land and oil spills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Many of these microbial reactions are shared with multicellular organisms, but due to the incredible diversity of types of microbes these organisms are able to deal with a far wider range of xenobiotics than multicellular organisms, and can degrade even <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochloride</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>A related problem for <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a> is <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-92">[92]</a></sup> Here, processes including <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a> and the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disulfide_bond" title="Disulfide bond">disulfide bonds</a> during <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">protein folding</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> These damaging oxidants are removed by <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant</a> metabolites such as <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a> and enzymes such as <a href="/wiki/Catalase" title="Catalase">catalases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peroxidase" title="Peroxidase">peroxidases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sies_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sies-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vertuani_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vertuani-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics_of_living_organisms">Thermodynamics of living organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Thermodynamics of living organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biological_thermodynamics" title="Biological thermodynamics">Biological thermodynamics</a></div>
<p>Living organisms must obey the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_thermodynamics" title="Laws of thermodynamics">laws of thermodynamics</a>, which describe the transfer of heat and <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)" title="Work (thermodynamics)">work</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> states that in any <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed system</a>, the amount of <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> (disorder) cannot decrease. Although living organisms' amazing complexity appears to contradict this law, life is possible as all organisms are <a href="/wiki/Open_system_(systems_theory)" title="Open system (systems theory)">open systems</a> that exchange matter and energy with their surroundings. Thus living systems are not in <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">equilibrium</a>, but instead are <a href="/wiki/Dissipative_system" title="Dissipative system">dissipative systems</a> that maintain their state of high complexity by causing a larger increase in the entropy of their environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The metabolism of a cell achieves this by coupling the <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_process" title="Spontaneous process">spontaneous processes</a> of catabolism to the non-spontaneous processes of anabolism. In <a href="/wiki/Non-equilibrium_thermodynamics" title="Non-equilibrium thermodynamics">thermodynamic</a> terms, metabolism maintains order by creating disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation_and_control">Regulation and control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Regulation and control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">Metabolic pathway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_control_analysis" title="Metabolic control analysis">Metabolic control analysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">Hormone</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulatory_enzymes" title="Regulatory enzymes">Regulatory enzymes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">Cell signaling</a></div>
<p>As the environments of most organisms are constantly changing, the reactions of metabolism must be finely <a href="/wiki/Control_theory" title="Control theory">regulated</a> to maintain a constant set of conditions within cells, a condition called <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Metabolic regulation also allows organisms to respond to signals and interact actively with their environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Two closely linked concepts are important for understanding how metabolic pathways are controlled. Firstly, the <i>regulation</i> of an enzyme in a pathway is how its activity is increased and decreased in response to signals. Secondly, the <i>control</i> exerted by this enzyme is the effect that these changes in its activity have on the overall rate of the pathway (the <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> through the pathway).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salter_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salter-101">[101]</a></sup> For example, an enzyme may show large changes in activity (<i>i.e.</i> it is highly regulated) but if these changes have little effect on the flux of a metabolic pathway, then this enzyme is not involved in the control of the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="745" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg/300px-Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg/450px-Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg/600px-Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Insulin_glucose_metabolism_ZP.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Effect of insulin on glucose uptake and metabolism.</b> Insulin binds to its receptor (1), which in turn starts many protein activation cascades (2). These include: translocation of Glut-4 transporter to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">plasma membrane</a> and influx of glucose (3), <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a> synthesis (4), <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> (5) and <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a> synthesis (6).</div></div></div>
<p>There are multiple levels of metabolic regulation. In intrinsic regulation, the metabolic pathway self-regulates to respond to changes in the levels of substrates or products; for example, a decrease in the amount of product can increase the <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">flux</a> through the pathway to compensate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salter_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Salter-101">[101]</a></sup> This type of regulation often involves <a href="/wiki/Allosteric_regulation" title="Allosteric regulation">allosteric regulation</a> of the activities of multiple enzymes in the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Extrinsic control involves a cell in a multicellular organism changing its metabolism in response to signals from other cells. These signals are usually in the form of soluble messengers such as <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Growth_factor" title="Growth factor">growth factors</a> and are detected by specific <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptors</a> on the cell surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> These signals are then transmitted inside the cell by <a href="/wiki/Second_messenger_system" title="Second messenger system">second messenger systems</a> that often involved the <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">phosphorylation</a> of proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>A very well understood example of extrinsic control is the regulation of glucose metabolism by the hormone <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Insulin is produced in response to rises in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_sugar" title="Blood sugar">blood glucose levels</a>. Binding of the hormone to <a href="/wiki/Insulin_receptor" title="Insulin receptor">insulin receptors</a> on cells then activates a cascade of <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase" title="Protein kinase">protein kinases</a> that cause the cells to take up glucose and convert it into storage molecules such as fatty acids and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The metabolism of glycogen is controlled by activity of <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylase" title="Phosphorylase">phosphorylase</a>, the enzyme that breaks down glycogen, and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen_synthase" title="Glycogen synthase">glycogen synthase</a>, the enzyme that makes it. These enzymes are regulated in a reciprocal fashion, with phosphorylation inhibiting glycogen synthase, but activating phosphorylase. Insulin causes glycogen synthesis by activating <a href="/wiki/Phosphatase" title="Phosphatase">protein phosphatases</a> and producing a decrease in the phosphorylation of these enzymes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_evolution" title="Molecular evolution">Molecular evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">Phylogenetics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_life_int.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="652" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tree_of_life_int.svg/400px-Tree_of_life_int.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tree_of_life_int.svg/600px-Tree_of_life_int.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Tree_of_life_int.svg/800px-Tree_of_life_int.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_of_life_int.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Evolutionary tree</a> showing the common ancestry of organisms from all three <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domains</a> of life. <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> are colored blue, <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotes</a> red, and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> green. Relative positions of some of the <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> included are shown around the tree.</div></div></div>
<p>The central pathways of metabolism described above, such as glycolysis and the citric acid cycle, are present in all <a href="/wiki/Three-domain_system" title="Three-domain system">three domains</a> of living things and were present in the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last universal common ancestor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SmithE_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-SmithE-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> This universal ancestral cell was <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">prokaryotic</a> and probably a <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">methanogen</a> that had extensive amino acid, nucleotide, carbohydrate and lipid metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The retention of these ancient pathways during later <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> may be the result of these reactions having been an optimal solution to their particular metabolic problems, with pathways such as glycolysis and the citric acid cycle producing their end products highly efficiently and in a minimal number of steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebenhoh_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ebenhoh-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cascante_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cascante-5">[5]</a></sup> The first pathways of enzyme-based metabolism may have been parts of <a href="/wiki/Purine" title="Purine">purine</a> nucleotide metabolism, while previous metabolic pathways were a part of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_world_hypothesis" title="RNA world hypothesis">RNA world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many models have been proposed to describe the mechanisms by which novel metabolic pathways evolve. These include the sequential addition of novel enzymes to a short ancestral pathway, the duplication and then divergence of entire pathways as well as the recruitment of pre-existing enzymes and their assembly into a novel reaction pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The relative importance of these mechanisms is unclear, but genomic studies have shown that enzymes in a pathway are likely to have a shared ancestry, suggesting that many pathways have evolved in a step-by-step fashion with novel functions created from pre-existing steps in the pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> An alternative model comes from studies that trace the evolution of proteins' structures in metabolic networks, this has suggested that enzymes are pervasively recruited, borrowing enzymes to perform similar functions in different metabolic pathways (evident in the <a href="/wiki/MANET_database" title="MANET database">MANET database</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> These recruitment processes result in an evolutionary enzymatic mosaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> A third possibility is that some parts of metabolism might exist as "modules" that can be reused in different pathways and perform similar functions on different molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as the evolution of new metabolic pathways, evolution can also cause the loss of metabolic functions. For example, in some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> metabolic processes that are not essential for survival are lost and preformed amino acids, nucleotides and carbohydrates may instead be scavenged from the <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Similar reduced metabolic capabilities are seen in <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiotic</a> organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigation_and_manipulation">Investigation and manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Investigation and manipulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_methods" title="Protein methods">Protein methods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">Proteomics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metabolomics" title="Metabolomics">Metabolomics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_network_modelling" title="Metabolic network modelling">Metabolic network modelling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="836" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png/300px-A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png/450px-A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png/600px-A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_thaliana_metabolic_network.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Metabolic_network" title="Metabolic network">Metabolic network</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">Enzymes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolite" title="Metabolite">metabolites</a> are shown as red squares and the interactions between them as black lines.</div></div></div>
<p>Classically, metabolism is studied by a <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">reductionist</a> approach that focuses on a single metabolic pathway. Particularly valuable is the use of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracers</a> at the whole-organism, tissue and cellular levels, which define the paths from precursors to final products by identifying radioactively labelled intermediates and products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The enzymes that catalyze these chemical reactions can then be <a href="/wiki/Protein_purification" title="Protein purification">purified</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_kinetics" title="Enzyme kinetics">kinetics</a> and responses to <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_inhibitor" title="Enzyme inhibitor">inhibitors</a> investigated. A parallel approach is to identify the small molecules in a cell or tissue; the complete set of these molecules is called the <a href="/wiki/Metabolome" title="Metabolome">metabolome</a>. Overall, these studies give a good view of the structure and function of simple metabolic pathways, but are inadequate when applied to more complex systems such as the metabolism of a complete cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>An idea of the complexity of the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_network" title="Metabolic network">metabolic networks</a> in cells that contain thousands of different enzymes is given by the figure showing the interactions between just 43 proteins and 40 metabolites to the right: the sequences of genomes provide lists containing anything up to 45,000 genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> However, it is now possible to use this genomic data to reconstruct complete networks of biochemical reactions and produce more <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holistic</a> mathematical models that may explain and predict their behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> These models are especially powerful when used to integrate the pathway and metabolite data obtained through classical methods with data on <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> from <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">proteomic</a> and <a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">DNA microarray</a> studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Using these techniques, a model of human metabolism has now been produced, which will guide future drug discovery and biochemical research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> These models are now used in <a href="/wiki/Network_theory" title="Network theory">network analysis</a>, to classify human diseases into groups that share common proteins or metabolites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bacterial metabolic networks are a striking example of <a href="/wiki/Bow_tie_(biology)" title="Bow tie (biology)">bow-tie</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15331224_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15331224-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID12874056_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID12874056-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PMID16916470_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-PMID16916470-130">[130]</a></sup> organization, an architecture able to input a wide range of nutrients and produce a large variety of products and complex macromolecules using a relatively few intermediate common currencies.
</p><p>A major technological application of this information is <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_engineering" title="Metabolic engineering">metabolic engineering</a>. Here, organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>, plants or <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> are genetically modified to make them more useful in <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and aid the production of <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drugs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> or industrial chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/1,3-Propanediol" title="1,3-Propanediol">1,3-propanediol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shikimic_acid" title="Shikimic acid">shikimic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> These genetic modifications usually aim to reduce the amount of energy used to produce the product, increase yields and reduce the production of wastes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_biochemistry" title="History of biochemistry">History of biochemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_molecular_biology" title="History of molecular biology">History of molecular biology</a></div>
<p>The term <i>metabolism</i> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> Μεταβολισμός – "Metabolismos" for "change", or "overthrow".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png/310px-Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png/465px-Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png/620px-Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle%27s_metabolism.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">Aristotle's metabolism</a> as an open flow model</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Parts_of_Animals" title="The Parts of Animals">The Parts of Animals</a></i> sets out enough details of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle%27s_biology" title="Aristotle's biology">his views on metabolism</a> for an open flow model to be made. He believed that at each stage of the process, materials from food were transformed, with heat being released as the <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical element</a> of fire, and residual materials being excreted as urine, bile, or faeces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> described metabolism in his 1260 AD work titled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Risalah_al-Kamiliyyah_fil_Siera_al-Nabawiyyah" title="Al-Risalah al-Kamiliyyah fil Siera al-Nabawiyyah">Al-Risalah al-Kamiliyyah fil Siera al-Nabawiyyah</a> (The Treatise of Kamil on the Prophet's Biography) which included the following phrase "Both the body and its parts are in a continuous state of dissolution and nourishment, so they are inevitably undergoing permanent change."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> 
The history of the scientific study of metabolism spans several centuries and has moved from examining whole animals in early studies, to examining individual metabolic reactions in modern biochemistry.  The first controlled <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a> in human metabolism were published by <a href="/wiki/Santorio_Santorio" title="Santorio Santorio">Santorio Santorio</a> in 1614 in his book <i>Ars de statica medicina</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> He described how he weighed himself before and after eating, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleeping" title="Sleeping">sleep</a>, working, sex, fasting, drinking, and excreting. He found that most of the food he took in was lost through what he called "insensible perspiration".
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SantoriosMeal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="310" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/SantoriosMeal.jpg/150px-SantoriosMeal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/SantoriosMeal.jpg/225px-SantoriosMeal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/SantoriosMeal.jpg/300px-SantoriosMeal.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SantoriosMeal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Santorio_Santorio" title="Santorio Santorio">Santorio Santorio</a> in his steelyard balance, from <i>Ars de statica medicina</i>, first published 1614</div></div></div>
<p>In these early studies, the mechanisms of these metabolic processes had not been identified and a <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vital force</a> was thought to animate living tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In the 19th century, when studying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> of sugar to <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> concluded that fermentation was catalyzed by substances within the yeast cells he called "ferments". He wrote that "alcoholic fermentation is an act correlated with the life and organization of the yeast cells, not with the death or putrefaction of the cells."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> This discovery, along with the publication by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedrich_Woehler" title="Friedrich Woehler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> in 1828 of a paper on the chemical synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> and is notable for being the first organic compound prepared from wholly inorganic precursors. This proved that the organic compounds and chemical reactions found in cells were no different in principle than any other part of chemistry.
</p><p>It was the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> at the beginning of the 20th century by <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Buchner" title="Eduard Buchner">Eduard Buchner</a> that separated the study of the chemical reactions of metabolism from the biological study of cells, and marked the beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The mass of biochemical knowledge grew rapidly throughout the early 20th century. One of the most prolific of these modern biochemists was <a href="/wiki/Hans_Adolf_Krebs" title="Hans Adolf Krebs">Hans Krebs</a> who made huge contributions to the study of metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> He discovered the urea cycle and later, working with <a href="/wiki/Hans_Kornberg" title="Hans Kornberg">Hans Kornberg</a>, the citric acid cycle and the glyoxylate cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornberg_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kornberg-65">[65]</a></sup> Modern biochemical research has been greatly aided by the development of new techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_diffraction" title="X-ray diffraction">X-ray diffraction</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NMR_spectroscopy" title="NMR spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotopic_labelling" title="Radioisotopic labelling">radioisotopic labelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron_microscope" title="Electron microscope">electron microscopy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a> simulations. These techniques have allowed the discovery and detailed analysis of the many molecules and metabolic pathways in cells.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metabolism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
